Chapter 1

Chapter 1

Father’s seal, cracked and brittle, crumbled under my thumb. His final letter. I expected blessings, a pious summation. Instead, the vellum whispers of a debt—not to God, but to a moneylender in Lyon. Our legacy is not land, but lies. And I, his dutiful son, must now choose which to honour.



My father, Sir Robert de Lacy, was in the undercroft, his voice a low, grinding stone against the squeal of a whetstone on a scythe blade. I could hear him from the courtyard. “A man who cannot sharpen his own tools is a man who cannot feed his own people. God gave you hands, not just to pray with.”

I stood in the archway, the autumn chill fingering the back of my neck. He was showing young Martin, the wheelwright’s son, the angle. His own hands, broad and scarred like old leather, engulfed the boy’s. He did not look up. “Well, Aelis? Is the roof fallen in, or do you just enjoy the draught?”

“A letter, Father.”

He finished the stroke, held the blade up to the dim light from the narrow window. “From?”

“The seal is blank.”

That made him look. He took the letter from me, his thumb rubbing over the wax. A flicker in his eyes, gone as quick as a fish in a murky pond. He broke the seal, unfolded the sheet. His lips moved slightly, soundlessly, as he read. He was not a fast reader. My mother had taught him, years ago, her finger tracing the words on the page by firelight. He had the letters, but they did not come easy to him; they were tools, like the scythe, to be used with effort.

He finished, refolded the sheet with precise, sharp creases, and tucked it inside his tunic. “Martin,” he said, his voice unchanged. “You see the bevel? Like that. Twenty more strokes, then you can go. Tell your father the new axle is holding.”

The boy nodded, terrified and proud. My father jerked his head at me and walked out, past the barrels of salt pork and the sacks of last year’s barley, up the stone steps into the hall. I followed. He went to the hearth, where a low fire fought the damp. He stood with his back to me, shoulders blocking the light.

“It is from your brother.”

My breath caught. Edric. Four years gone. A handful of letters in that time, short, dutiful things sent from London, then Calais, then places with names we could barely pronounce. He was in the household of Sir John de Mandeville, a lord with interests across the Channel. A great opportunity, his first letter had said. A chance to rise.

“What does he say?”

Father held the letter out to the firelight again, though I knew he had memorized every word. “He is coming home. Sir John is dead. The household is dissolved. He has… prospects. He arrives before Martinmas.”

“Prospects?” The word felt strange in the hall, with its smoke-blackened beams and the smell of damp rushes.

“He does not say.” Father finally turned. His face, weathered and square as a block of our local granite, was unreadable. “He writes a fine hand now. Better than the priest’s.”

There was an accusation in that. I said nothing. Edric’s letters to me had been different. Less fine. In one, he’d written, Aelis, this court life is a tapestry seen from the back. All knots and loose ends and the occasional flash of colour that means nothing. Father would call it vanity. He is not entirely wrong. But that was a year ago. The last one had been all about the price of Burgundian wool.

“We must prepare,” Father said. “The east chamber. It has been shut up. It will need airing. He will be used to… finer things.”

“He will be used to home,” I said, too quickly.

He looked at me then, a long, measuring look. “Will he?” He walked to the trestle table, picked up a ledger, opened it. The conversation was over. I was dismissed to the duties of airing and preparing, to the world of linens and floor rushes and the faint, musty smell of a room long closed.

But the letter’s smell lingered on my hands. Not woodsmoke. Something else. Ink, yes, but a sharper, more metallic ink than Father used for his accounts. And beneath it, the ghost of something sweet and cloying. Perfume. A courtier’s letter, trying to mask its journey.
  - The east chamber was a tomb of memories. I pushed the heavy oak door open, the iron strap cold under my palm. Dust motes danced in the slender lance of grey light from the arrow-slit window. Edric had left at seventeen, a boy with my father’s build but our mother’s restless eyes. He’d taken little: a spare tunic, his knife, the copy of Boethius’s Consolation that Mother had left to him. The room had been stripped after, the chest empty, the pallet rolled and stored. It held only a carved wooden knight on the windowsill, one leg chipped from when he’d dropped it as a child, and the chill.

Mab, the old housekeeper, came puffing up behind me with a bundle of fresh straw. “So the young master returns,” she said, her tone as dry as the rushes we were about to sweep out. “With his fine manners and his empty purse, no doubt.”

“Mab.”

“Well, it’s the way of it, Mistress Aelis. They go off to see the world and come back thinking the world is in their pocket. Only it’s usually just a hole.” She began beating the old pallet with a stick, raising storms of dust. “He’ll find us much the same, I warrant. Though your father’s back is stiffer and the millstream floods more often.”

I worked beside her, shaking out the mothballed woollen blanket, our breath making faint clouds in the cold air. My thoughts were not on dust or floods. They were on the letter’s tone, which I had not seen but could infer from the set of my father’s shoulders as he read. It had been a report, not a son’s plea. I am coming home.

“Will he stay, Mab?”

She stopped her beating, leaned on the stick. Her face, a web of wrinkles, was shrewd. “That is not the question, child. The question is, will the manor hold him? A bird that’s flown beyond the wood finds its old nest very small.”

“It’s his home.”

“It is his father’s house,” she corrected gently. “And you are its steward now, in all but name. That is a different thing.”

I felt a prick of irritation, hot and sudden. “I do not grudge him his place.”

“I did not say you did. I said it is a different thing.” She resumed her beating. “Letters are poor containers for a man’s heart. They carry only the words that fit. We shall see what the man himself brings.”

By evening, the room was habitable. A fire crackled in the small hearth, chasing the grave-damp back into the stones. The blanket lay smooth on the fresh straw pallet. The wooden knight stood sentinel on the sill. It looked like a room waiting for a stranger.

At supper, Father was silent, methodically working his way through a bowl of pottage. The silence was not unusual, but tonight it had a density, a focused quality. He was marshalling his thoughts, preparing for a siege.

“We will kill the Martinmas pig early,” he said, finally. “The one from the acorn wood. The meat is better.”

“A welcome feast,” I said.

“A necessity. He will have a man, at least. Perhaps two. They will need feeding.” He sopped up the last of his pottage with a piece of coarse bread. “We must show good management. The accounts. The stores. You will ensure everything is in order.”

“Everything is always in order, Father.”

He grunted, not quite agreement. “Edric has been where order is a performance. A painted screen. He will look behind it.” He pushed his bowl away. “I will write to the abbot. We have the wool clip ready. It is a good yield. He should see that.”

He was talking about assets, about demonstrations of stability. This was not a father preparing for a son, but a lord preparing for an audit. My heart ached, suddenly, for both of them. For Edric, whose homecoming was being tallied like sacks of grain, and for my father, who knew no other language of love but that of provision and solidity.

Later, in the quiet of the hall with just the dying fire, I took out my own writing box. A small, precious thing of inlaid wood. Inside, nestled in a fold of cloth, were Edric’s letters to me. I untied the ribbon, spread them out. The progression was there, in the paper, in the hand. The early ones on rough, thick stuff, the words sprawling and energetic. The later ones on finer, smoother parchment, the script compact, controlled, less him. The last one was all markets, tariffs, the favour of a certain Lord R.

I dipped my quill. The ink was my own, oak-gall and iron, without perfume.

Brother,

Your news is received. The east chamber is aired. The Martinmas pig is condemned. Father sharpens scythes and studies ledgers. The manor is as you remember, yet not. I am no longer the girl who chased you with frogs. I keep the accounts now. Mab says a bird that has flown beyond the wood finds its old nest small. I pray she is a foolish old woman.

Travel safely. The roads are foul with autumn.

Your sister,
Aelis

I sanded the page, folded it. I would not send it. There was no time, and no sure way to find him on the road. It was practice. It was speaking into the silence he had left, and the new, more complicated silence his return was bringing. I held it to my nose. It smelled of oak-gall, of home. A blunt, honest smell. I wondered which he would prefer now.

Outside, an early wind rattled the shutters. It was a sound from childhood, a lullaby. But tonight it sounded like someone trying to get in.

Chapter 2

Chapter 2

He arrived on a day of brittle, cloudless cold, the kind that makes the world seem etched in glass. We heard them first: the jingle of harness, the hollow clop of hooves on the frozen track, a voice laughing—a bright, foreign sound against the grey woods. I was in the storeroom, counting wax seals for the wool sacks, my fingers numb. The sounds stopped at the gate. A pause. Then the creak of the hinge as someone pushed it open—a confident, unfamiliar push, not old Will’s grudging drag.

I smoothed my apron, tucked a stray piece of hair under my coif, and walked out into the courtyard.

There were three of them. Edric sat a tall, sleek bay rounsey, a beast that cost more than our year’s charcoal. He was wrapped in a cloak of good, dark blue wool, trimmed with fox fur. Not the boy who left. His face was leaner, the softness planed away, leaving angles. A neatly trimmed beard, fashionable, framed his mouth. He saw me and his eyes—still Mother’s eyes, a restless grey—lit with recognition. But the smile that followed was a performance, a thing of practised warmth.

“Aelis! By God, you’ve grown into a woman.” He swung down from the saddle with an easy grace my father never possessed. He clasped my arms, kissed both my cheeks. He smelled of leather, cold air, and that same faint, sweet perfume from the letter.

“Welcome home, Edric.”

Behind him, the two men dismounted. One was older, grizzled, with the watchful stillness of a soldier. He nodded at me, his hand resting on his sword hilt. The other was a youth, pale and pimpled, who scurried to hold the horses’ heads.

“This is Hugh,” Edric said, gesturing to the soldier. “My… companion. And that’s Piers. They’ve been with me through some troubles.” He said it lightly, but Hugh’s eyes were scanning the courtyard, the walls, the roof, assessing defensibility, judging worth.

Father emerged from the hall. He stood on the steps, not coming forward. He had put on his best tunic, the green one with the faded de Lacy hawk on the breast. He looked solid, immovable, a part of the stone itself.

Edric’s performance faltered for a heartbeat. Then he strode forward, knelt—a perfect, courtly kneel, one knee on the cold cobbles, head bowed. “Father.”

“Get up,” Father said. His voice was gruff. “The ground is frozen. You’ll take a chill.” It was as close to an endearment as he could manage. He put a hand on Edric’s shoulder, raised him. They looked at each other, eye to eye now, for the first time in four years. Two men, strangers in familiar skin.

“You look well,” Father said.

“And you, sir. The manor looks prosperous.”

“It holds.”

The silence stretched, filled by the snorting of the horses. Mab appeared in the doorway, wiping her hands on her skirts. Her sharp eyes took in the scene in one glance. “Shall I have the beasts seen to, Sir Robert? And the men can warm themselves in the kitchen.”

Father nodded, grateful for the practicalities. “Yes. See to it.” He turned back to Edric. “Come inside. Your sister has seen to your room.”

The feast that night was a strained pantomime. The acorn pig was roasted, the ale was the best of the autumn brewing, and Mab had produced a spiced wine she hoarded like dragon’s gold. We sat at the high table: Father at the head, Edric to his right, me to his left. Hugh and Piers were at a lower trestle with the household. The hall was warm, smoky, loud with the crackle of the fire and the murmur of our people stealing glances at the returned heir.

Edric spoke of his travels. Of the court at Westminster—“all gilt and gossip”—of the markets of Calais, of the great churches of Paris. His words were colourful, fluent. He described a tournament, the crash of lance on shield, the roar of the crowd. Father listened, chewing his meat slowly, his eyes on his son’s face.

“And Sir John?” Father asked, during a lull. “A good lord?”

Edric’s smile tightened at the corners. “A shrewd one. He knew the price of everything. His death was… sudden. A fever. The household was like a clock without its mainspring. Fell apart.” He took a drink of wine. “But it presented opportunities. I have made connections, Father. Men of influence. Lord Raby, for one. He has lands in Aquitaine. He needs reliable men who understand both England and the Continent.”

“Aquitaine,” Father repeated, the word exotic and dangerous in his mouth. “That is a long way to go to collect rents.”

“It is not about rents,” Edric said, a flicker of impatience in his eyes. He smoothed it over. “It is about administration. Trade. The future is in networks, in alliances that cross borders. Not in… well.” He gestured vaguely, taking in the hall, the fire, us.

“Not in what?” I asked. My voice sounded small.

He turned his performer’s smile on me. “Not in standing still, little sister. The world is moving. A man must move with it, or be left behind as a curiosity.” He said it kindly, but the wound was there. We were the curiosities.

Father put his knife down. It clattered on the wooden plate. “This manor has stood for two hundred years. Through plague and famine and war. It stands by not chasing every new wind that blows from London. It stands by the strength of its stone and the sweat of its people. That is not standing still. That is holding the line.”

Edric held his gaze. The hall had gone quiet. “Holding a line is a defensive strategy, Father. It assumes the world will attack. I speak of building. Of expanding.”

“You speak of overreaching.”

They stared at each other across the roast pig, the two philosophies of the age laid bare on a trestle table in a smoky hall. Feudal solidity versus opportunistic ambition. The land versus the deal.

Hugh, at the lower table, watched this with the calm of a man who has seen families fracture over less. Piers looked terrified.

I stood up. “The journey has been long. You must be tired, Edric. Your room is ready.” It was a woman’s intervention, a retreat into domesticity. Both men looked at me, momentarily united in their irritation at the interruption. Then Father nodded, a sharp jerk of his head.

“Yes. Go. We will talk of… practicalities… tomorrow.”

Later, I brought a cup of warmed, watered wine to Edric’s chamber. He was standing at the arrow-slit, looking out at the blackness. He had shed his fine cloak and tunic. In his linen shirt, he looked younger, more like the brother I remembered.

“Thank you, Aelis.” He took the cup, sipped. “God, this is good. The wine at court tastes like vinegar with ideas above its station.”

“We are simple folk here.”

He heard the edge in my voice. “I did not mean—”

“I know what you meant.” I walked to the hearth, stirred the fire with a poker. “Father is not your enemy, Edric.”

“I know that.” He sighed, ran a hand through his hair. “But he sees an enemy in every new thought. He thinks I’ve been corrupted.”

“Have you?”

He looked at me, startled. Then he laughed, a real laugh this time, short and bitter. “Probably. But corruption is just the polish another man puts on the world. I’ve learned how things work. Truly work. It’s not about oaths and honour, Aelis. It’s about debt and favour. It’s about parchment.” He set the cup down, went to a leather satchel by the bed. He pulled out a document, a thick roll sealed with a great, impressive dollop of red wax. “This is from Lord Raby. A letter of introduction to his seneschal in Bordeaux. It is worth more than ten horses.”

“What does it promise?”

“It promises a hearing. An opportunity to prove my worth. To manage a portion of his holdings there. A percentage of the profits.” His eyes were alight now, with the cold fire of calculation. “This is what I brought home, Aelis. Not just fine clothes. A future.”

“And what of de Lacy’s future? This manor? These people?”

He waved a hand. “This will always be here. This is the foundation. But a man cannot live his life in the foundation. He must build the house upon it.” He saw my expression. “I will see Father right. I will see you right. A marriage, Aelis. A good one. To a knight’s son, perhaps. Not some provincial nobody.”

He said it as a gift. I felt it as a sentence. I was now a “practicality” to be managed.

“You sound like Sir John,” I said quietly. “Knowing the price of everything.”

The words hung in the air. The friendly light died from his eyes. He carefully rerolled the parchment. “Go to bed, Aelis. You have your accounts to keep in the morning.”

I left him there, with his fire and his future on parchment. As I walked the cold corridor back to my own small room, I passed my father’s chamber. A line of yellow light showed under his door. He was awake. Not reading. Just sitting in the dark, I imagined, holding the line against the new wind blowing under his own roof.

Down in the courtyard, a horse whickered. Hugh, I thought, checking the perimeter. A man used to troubles. I wondered what troubles Edric had brought home, tangled in the fine wool of his cloak and the sweet smell of his ink.

Chapter 3

Chapter 3

The argument began over breakfast, as the worst ones do. It was about a ditch.

Old Will, his face like a worried apple, stood shifting from foot to foot before the high table. “It’s the boundary ditch, Sir Robert, ‘twixt our lower field and the demesne strip by the copse. It’s silted up. Last night’s rain, it flooded over. Young Martin’s barley is under water.”

Father nodded, spooning up his porridge. “Take two men and the ox-cart. Clear it. Use the spoil to bank the side by Martin’s plot.”

“Aye, sir.” Will turned to go.

“Wait.” Edric put down his knife. He had been quiet, breaking his bread into precise pieces. “That ditch has been a problem for years. It silts every spring and autumn. Clearing it is a stopgap.”

Father’s spoon paused. “It is the work that needs doing.”

“It is wasted labour,” Edric said, his voice calm, reasonable. The courtier’s voice. “The course is wrong. It follows the old hedge line, but the land has sunk there since my grandfather’s time. You need to re-cut it. Fifty yards to the east, along the natural slope. It would drain the whole lower field, not just Martin’s strip. Permanently.”

The hall was silent. Will looked desperately from father to son. I held my breath. To question a father’s management on his own land, before his own people, on his second morning home…

Father set his spoon down carefully. “To re-cut it would take ten men a week. We have shearing in a fortnight. The hay will be ready after that. When would you have this done? In the hypothetical month of leisure I have hidden in my coffers?”

“Hire the labour,” Edric said, as if it were the most obvious thing in the world. “From the village. Pay them in coin or in kind from the increased yield next year. It is an investment.”

“We do not hire what we can do ourselves. That is the road to dependency and idleness. The village has its own work.”

“And this would be their work. For a wage. It puts coin in their purse and improves your land. Everyone gains.”

“The land is not improved by impatience,” Father said, his voice growing low. “It is improved by care. By knowing it. That ditch has carried water away from that field since before you were a thought. It is not the ditch’s fault the rains are heavier.”

“It is not about fault. It is about efficiency.” Edric leaned forward. “This is what I am trying to show you. A new way of thinking. We are not just subsistence farmers, holding off the wolf. We are managers of an estate. We should be increasing its value, not just maintaining it against decay.”

Father stood up. The bench scraped loud on the flagstones. “I know the value of my land. It is not counted only in bushels per acre. It is in the loyalty of the men who work it, who know I will not gamble their winter bread on a new way of thinking. It is in the fact that when the floods come, or the frost, we dig out together. We do not hire our solidarity.” He looked at Will. “Clear the ditch. As I said.”

Will fled. Edric sat back, his face a mask of cool disappointment. He did not look at Father. He looked at the wall, as if reading a disappointing letter posted there.

“You see only the cost of the labour,” he said, almost to himself. “Not the cost of the lost opportunity.”

“And you see the land as a ledger,” Father shot back. “It is not. It is a body. You do not hack a new channel in a body because the old one is slow. You tend it.”

The fracture was open now, a raw, clean split down the middle of our world. The rest of the day passed in a tense, brittle quiet. Father went to the sheep pens. Edric announced he would ride to the village with Hugh, to “see the lie of the land.” I knew he was going to assess the available labour, to price his hypothetical ditch.

I retreated to the steward’s room, a small closet off the hall where the ledgers were kept. I opened the great book, the one my father could read but not love. The columns of figures were my solace. They were neutral. A bushel of rye was a bushel of rye. It did not argue.

I was checking the wool clip tally against the abbot’s promised price when the door opened. It was Hugh. He filled the doorway, his manner apologetic but insistent.

“Mistress Aelis. A word?”

I nodded, wary. He stepped in, closing the door softly behind him. He did not look like a soldier up close; he looked like a tired carpenter, his face lined with practical worries.

“It’s about your brother,” he said, keeping his voice low. “He won’t ask. His pride. But I’ve sworn to see him safe, and safety is more than a sword can provide.”

“What is it?”

“The opportunities he speaks of… with Lord Raby. They are real. But they are not gifts. They are advances.” He met my eyes. “Sir John died deeply in debt. To moneylenders. Men in London, with no coats of arms but long memories and sharp knives. Your brother was his clerk. His favoured clerk. Some of those debts… the perception is they cling to him.”

A cold knot formed in my stomach. “How much?”

Hugh shook his head. “More than this manor sees in five years. It is not a legal burden, not yet. It is a shadow. The kind of shadow that discourages other patrons, that makes men reluctant to do business. Lord Raby’s letter is a chance to step into a new light, far away. To outrun it, and to earn enough to maybe… dampen the shadow.”

“So he needs this,” I said. “Desperately.”

“He needs to be seen as a man of prospects, not a man pursued. His vision for this place… it is not just ambition. It is a need to generate capital. Quickly. To show the world—and those men—that he is a rising stream, not a stagnant pool where debts collect.” Hugh glanced at the ledger. “He will push your father. He must. Forgive him his manner. It is fear that drives him, not disrespect.”

I understood now. The performance, the impatience, the need to turn everything into a calculable gain. It was the panic of a man treading water with weights on his ankles. My irritation melted into a sour, helpless pity.

“Why tell me?”

“Because you are the stone in the centre of this ford,” he said simply. “Your father stands on one bank, your brother on the other. The current is getting swift. Someone must hold fast, or both will be swept away.” He gave a short, stiff bow. “Thank you for your time, mistress.”

He left. I stared at the columns of numbers. They swam before my eyes. They were not neutral anymore. They were the thin barrier between my brother and ruin, between my father’s world and a vortex of distant, impersonal greed.

That evening, Edric did not come down for supper. Piers said he was tired from the ride. Father ate in stony silence. After, I took a tray to the east chamber: bread, cheese, a slice of cold pork.

He was at the small table, writing. Not with the perfumed ink, but with my plain oak-gall. He looked up, his face drawn.

“Hugh told you,” he said. It wasn’t a question.

“Some of it.”

He laughed, a hollow sound. “My faithful hound. He thinks you are a peacemaker.” He put the quill down. “There is no peace to be made, Aelis. Only a choice. Father’s way leads to a slow, honourable decline. Mine leads through a dangerous gate. But on the other side of that gate, there is a chance for something more than just… survival.”

“He sees your gate as the destruction of everything he has built.”

“And I see his wall as my tomb.” He looked at the letter he was writing. It was to Lord Raby’s seneschal. “I cannot stay here, Aelis. Not like this. But I cannot launch myself to Bordeaux without resources. I need to show I can generate wealth. That ditch… it was a symbol. To him, it was disobedience. To me, it was the first step off the cliff.”

I set the tray down. “What will you do?”

He looked at me, and for a fleeting second, the boy was there, scared and determined. “What I must. There is the wool clip. The abbot’s price is good, but the Flemish merchants in London would pay twenty percent more. I have the contacts. I could take it, sell it, return the abbot’s price to Father with a profit on top. My share would be my seed corn for Bordeaux.”

It was a betrayal. The abbot was our oldest patron, a handshake agreement over generations. It was stability. It was the feudal web Father believed in.

“Father will never agree.”

“Then he must not know until it is done.” Edric’s voice was quiet, final. “The wagons are loaded. They leave for the abbey tomorrow. But the road to London forks off two miles past the village. Hugh knows a factor there. It can be done in a week. Father will have his money, plus more. The manor benefits. I get my chance. No one is harmed.”

“Except trust,” I whispered.

“Trust is a luxury for the secure,” he said, and there was no bitterness now, just a bleak certainty. “I am writing to the seneschal tonight. I will tell him to expect me by Easter, with capital and a proven head for profit.” He picked up the quill again. “You should go, Aelis. You should not be part of this.”

But I was part of it. The moment Hugh spoke to me, I became the stone in the ford. I knew. To not tell Father was to betray him. To tell him was to destroy Edric.

I left him there, writing his future with my ink. Downstairs, Father was at the hearth, staring into the flames, sharpening a knife with slow, rhythmic strokes. Scritch. Scritch. The sound of holding the line.

I stood in the shadows, the cold of the corridor at my back, the heat of the fire ahead. The letter was in my brother’s hand. The debt was in the shadows. The wool was in the carts. And I was in the middle, holding the secret, feeling the current pull at my feet.



---

Chapter 4

Chapter 4

The decision did not come like a thunderclap. It settled in the bones, like the deep chill of the undercroft, a certainty that was more absence than presence. I did not sleep. I lay in the narrow bed, listening to the manor house speak its night language—the groan of timber, the scuttle of a mouse in the wattle, the distant, rhythmic scritch-scritch from below that meant Father was still at the hearth, still sharpening. He was wearing down the blade to a filament of steel. He was wearing down the night.

By the time the first grey light bled through the oiled parchment of my window, the secret had crystallized. It was no longer a thing I carried; it was a lens through which I saw the world, distorting every familiar line. The smell of baking bread from the kitchens was the smell of normalcy, a lie. The call of the goose-girl driving her flock to the common was a call from a life I was no longer in.

I found Hugh by the well in the courtyard, drawing water for the horses. His sleeves were rolled past his elbows, forearms corded with the effort, the water slopping over the bucket’s lip and darkening the beaten earth. He did not look at me, but his shoulders tightened, a hound catching a scent.

“The carts,” I said. My voice was rough with the night’s silence.

He set the bucket down. Water lapped at his boots. “Loaded. The oxen are yoked. They wait for your father’s word to depart for the abbey.”

“And the fork? Two miles past the village?”

Now he looked at me. His eyes were the colour of a winter stream, all surface clarity and cold depth. “You know of it.”

“I know of it.”

He picked up the bucket, hefting its weight as if testing it. “The factor will be at the Crooked Ash. He has the silver for the abbot’s price, and a letter of credit for the Flemish surplus. It is arranged.”

“It is betrayal.”

“It is commerce,” he said, and the word was foreign on his lips, a hard, city word. “Your brother’s future for a week’s detour. The manor loses nothing. Gains, even.”

“It loses its word.”

Hugh’s mouth twisted, something that was not quite a smile. “A word is a breath. Land is land. Silver is silver.” He hoisted the bucket. “He asked you not to be part of it.”

“He made me part of it when he told me.” I stepped closer, the damp earth cool through my thin soles. “You will drive the lead cart?”

“I will.”

“Then I am coming with you.”

He stilled. The water in the bucket went quiet. “No.”

“To the fork. No further. I will see it done. I will be the witness.”

“To what end? To share the guilt? To have a stone to throw at him later?” He shook his head. “This is men’s business. Sharp business. It is no place for you.”

The dismissal was a physical thing, a hand on my chest pushing me back into the role of the steward’s daughter, the observer. I felt the old anger, the one that smelled of wet wool and distant woodsmoke, rise in my throat. “It is my family’s business. My brother is stealing my father’s honour and selling it in London. That makes it mine. You need a reason for my presence. Tell my father I am taking the opportunity of the cart’s escort to visit the anchorite at the crossroads oak. He will allow it. He approves of piety.”

Hugh studied me, and I saw him weighing it, not as a man weighs a woman’s whim, but as a soldier weighs a variable in a field of battle. The anchorite, old Mother Mildred, was a valid pretext. My father did approve. It was just far enough.

“And at the fork?” he asked.

“I will get down. I will walk to the oak. You will turn towards London. I will pray for you both.” The words tasted of ash.

He held my gaze for a long moment, then gave a single, curt nod. It was not agreement between allies. It was an acknowledgement of a new, unwelcome fact, like a crack in a foundation stone. “Be ready after terce. Do not bring much.”

He left me there, the water from his bucket leaving a dark, seeping trail back towards the stables. The plan was a shoddy thing, knotted together from fear and necessity. It would hold for a mile. It might hold for two.
  - My father broke his fast on ale and yesterday’s bread, his knife scraping hard crust. Edric was absent. The tension in the hall was a high, thin wire, humming. I could see Father listening for his son’s step on the stair, the absence of it another stone on the scale of his disappointment.

“The wool train is ready,” I said, pouring his ale. “Hugh awaits your command.”

“Good.” He did not look up. “The abbot expects it by noon. Let there be no delay.” He took a long drink, his throat working. “The world runs on obligation, Aelis. On the timely meeting of expectations. A day late is a promise broken. A promise broken is a crack in the wall. And the wind always finds the crack.”

Scritch. Scritch. I could still hear it in my head.

“I would ask a boon,” I said, the words too loud in the quiet hall.

He looked up then, his eyes like chips of flint in the leather of his face. “What boon?”

“The carts pass the crossroads oak. I wish to visit the anchorite, to take her some comforts from our stores. A sack of meal. Some apples. I can ride on the lead cart, return with Hugh on his way back from the abbey.” I kept my hands still, folded at my waist. A steward’s daughter’s hands.

He considered. Piety was a currency he understood. A daughter’s spiritual concern was a reflection of a well-ordered household. A crack sealed. “Mother Mildred is a holy woman. Her prayers have value. Take the white apples from the north orchard. And the good meal, not the sweepings.” He returned to his bread. “Do not tarry. Hugh’s business is my business, and I will not have it delayed for women’s chatter.”

“Yes, Father.”

It was done. The first step. As I turned to go, he spoke again, his voice lower. “Has your brother spoken to you?”

Every muscle in my back locked. “He was in the scriptorium last night. Writing.”

“Writing.” The word was ground out. “To his new master, no doubt. Laying his service at the feet of a man who counts coins, not souls.” He pushed his platter away. “Let him write. The wool will be delivered. The abbot’s silver will fill our chest. The world will turn as it should. He will learn that his dreams are smoke, and that this land is the only truth.”

He was speaking to the hearth, to the ghosts of his own father and his father before him. I was already a ghost in the room, slipping away, carrying the lie like a stolen sacrament.
  - The wool train was three carts, long and heavy, their beds piled high with greasy, sack-clothed bales that smelled of lanolin and pasture. The oxen were vast, patient creatures, their breath pluming in the cool morning air, their great heads bowed under the yokes. Hugh sat on the bench of the lead cart, the reins loose in his hands. He did not offer a hand up. I placed my foot on the wheel hub and pulled myself onto the sack of meal beside him, my basket of apples in my lap.

We lurched into motion with a groan of wood and a creak of iron-shod wheels. The manor gates passed over us, the shadow of the arch a cold stripe across my hands. I did not look back. To look back was to waver.

For a mile, we rode in silence, the only sounds the crunch of wheels on the track, the snort of the oxen, the distant cry of a curlew. The land unfolded, familiar as my own hand—the strip fields, the common with its browsing geese, the dark smear of the wood beyond.

“He will ruin himself,” Hugh said suddenly, his eyes fixed on the oxen’s swaying backs. “Not with this. This is clean enough. But with the wanting. It is a fire in him. It will consume what little he has.”

“You help him stoke it.”

“I served his grandfather. I serve this land. Your brother… he sees a path out. I am clearing the brambles from that path. That is all.” He flicked the reins. “Your father sees a circle, unbroken. Your brother sees a line, leading away. Neither sees the cliff that edges both.”

“And you?” I asked.

“I see the weather. I know when to lower my head and when to run for shelter.” He glanced at me, a quick, assessing look. “You are running for no shelter. You are walking into the weather.”

I had no answer. He was right. The cart rolled on, leaving the manor lands, passing the last boundary stone. The village huddled ahead, a clutch of thatch and smoke. We clattered through its single street, past the tanner’s yard where the boy had come from, past the smithy ringing with hammer blows. Faces turned to watch the lord’s wool pass. I kept my eyes ahead, feeling like an imposter, a hollow girl playing at consequence.

Past the village, the land grew wilder, the track rutted by spring rains. The woods crowded closer, their branches fingering the grey sky. The fork was ahead, marked by a lightning-blasted willow. My heart began a slow, thick drumming against my ribs.

Hugh pulled the oxen to a halt. The other two carts ground to a stop behind us. The silence, after the long rumble, was immense.

“The oak is a half-mile down the left path,” he said, his voice devoid of inflection. “We take the right.”

I sat, clutching my basket. The plan, so clear in the courtyard, now seemed a child’s fantasy. To get down here, to walk away, was to cut the rope and set the boat adrift. I would be alone with my knowledge, and the deed would be done beyond my sight, a story told to me later in guilty fragments.

“I am coming to London,” I said.

The words hung in the damp air. Hugh did not move. “No.”

“You cannot stop me. I will follow. I have no horse, but I have feet. I will be a greater danger wandering alone on the road than I will be on your cart. My father will flay you alive if I come to harm.” I heard the steel in my own voice, a stranger’s voice. “You said this was men’s business. Then let me be a ledger-keep. Let me see the numbers written. Let me be the witness, not to the turn, but to the whole road.”

He turned to me then, and for the first time, I saw something other than grim competence in his face. It was a kind of weary respect, edged with annoyance. “You are your father’s daughter. And your brother’s sister. A dangerous mix.” He looked up the right-hand fork, a track that wound into deeper woodland, away from the known world. He sighed, a long, slow exhalation. “Get in the back. Under the sacking. You will be jolted raw and smell of sheep for a week. If we are stopped, you are a runaway. I found you on the road. I know nothing.”

It was not agreement. It was capitulation to a greater nuisance. I did not care. I scrambled over the seat, into the cart-bed, burrowing between the rough, greasy bales. Hugh threw a piece of stiff sacking over me. The world became a dark, close prison, reeking of animal and earth. The cart lurched again, turning onto the right-hand path.

The journey became a timeless, jolting agony. Every stone, every rut, travelled up through the wooden boards and into my bones. The wool fibres dusted the air, catching in my throat. I lost all sense of direction, of distance. There was only the grind of the wheels, the slow, steady plod of the oxen, and the tight knot of dread in my stomach. I had crossed the fork. There was no returning to the oak, to piety, to the daughter I was supposed to be. I was in the belly of the betrayal, being carried towards its market.

Time stretched and snapped. I must have dozed, for I woke to the cart stopping and the sound of voices. Not Hugh’s. Other men’s. Rough, clipped tones. My heart hammered against the wool bale. We could not be at London yet. The factor? Bandits?

I heard Hugh’s voice, calm, steady. “A toll for this road? Since when?”

“Since now,” came a reply, thick with phlegm. “The bridge at Stoney Brook is washed out. This is the only passable way. Our way. Our toll.”

“I carry the wool of Sir Robert de Lacy. He does not pay tolls to road-wolves.”

A laugh, ugly and sharp. “He does today. Or his wool takes a swim in the mire.”

I held my breath. The sacking over me seemed as thin as linen. I heard the creak of leather, the soft shush of a blade being drawn—not Hugh’s, from the sound. It was a longer, heavier sound.

“A silver penny per cart,” the rough voice said. “Or we lighten your load. Starting with you.”

There was a pause. I could picture Hugh, weighing the odds. Three carts, two other drivers. How many bandits? The wrong decision here meant more than silver; it meant blood, and wool sunk in bog, and a story that could never be explained.

“A penny is steep for a washed-out bridge,” Hugh said, his voice closer now, as if he had stepped down. “But a man must be compensated for his labour in repairing a road. Here is three pence. For the road.”

The clink of coin. A grunt. “Wise man. You’ll find the way clear another mile on. Mind the ruts.”

The cart moved again. I lay shaking in my wool tomb, the reality of the world outside my father’s walls a cold slap. This was the sharp business Hugh had spoken of. Not just ledgers and factors, but men with knives who made their own laws between the trees. Edric’s dream was paved with this. My father’s world was built to keep this out. And I was somewhere in the grimy middle, paying a toll in fear.

We stopped again as dusk began to bleed the colour from the sky. Hugh pulled back the sacking. The air was cold and clean, smelling of damp leaves and woodsmoke. We were in a clearing by a stream. The other drivers—old Tom and young John—were unhitching the oxen. They glanced at me as I emerged, stiff and filthy, but asked no questions. Hugh’s word was law on the road.

“We camp here,” Hugh said, handing me a waterskin. “London is two days’ hard travel yet. We’ve left the abbey road far behind.” He studied me in the fading light. “The toll was three pence. You owe your brother a penny.”

It was a joke, but his eyes were not joking. They were measuring the cost. I drank the water, my throat parched. “What would you have done if they had asked for more?”

“Given it. Then followed them and taken it back.” He turned to build a fire. “The wool is the thing. It must get through.”

We ate a spare meal of hard cheese and drier bread. The fire was small, a secretive thing, its light not reaching the encircling trees. I sat with my back against a wheel, the events of the day settling into me like sediment. The boldness that had seized me at the fork was gone, drained away by the jolting cart and the bandit’s voice. What was I doing here? I was no ledger-keep. I was a girl with a head full of stories, who had mistaken the hearing of a secret for the strength to bear its weight. I had left my father in his hall, sharpening his knives against the coming dark. I had left my brother in his scriptorium, writing his future with stolen ink. And I was here, in a damp clearing, an accomplice to the fracture between them. A witness, yes. But to what? To my own family’s unravelling.

The doubt was a cold stone in my gut. This was not my mission. It was Edric’s desperation, Hugh’s pragmatism. I had inserted myself into the crack, thinking I could somehow hold the walls together, but I was only widening it. I thought of Mother Mildred in her cell at the oak, touching the stones worn smooth by a lifetime of prayer. Certainty. A terrible, narrow certainty. I had none.

“He will hate me for this,” I said into the firelight, not meaning to speak aloud.

Hugh, honing a stick to a point with his belt knife, did not look up. “Who?”

“Edric. When he sees me in London. He wanted me clear of it. He wanted to do this clean, alone. My presence… it makes it real. It makes it a family betrayal, not a business venture.”

“It was always a family betrayal,” Hugh said, his knife moving in short, sure strokes. “You just chose your side.”

“Did I?” The question was a plea. “I haven’t told Father. That is a side. But I haven’t stopped Edric. That is another.”

“You are in the middle,” he said, echoing my own thought from the night before. “The middle is no place to be. It is where you get crushed.” He threw the sharpened stick into the fire. It hissed. “Sleep. The oxen need less rest than you think. We move at first light.”

I lay wrapped in my cloak, listening to the stream and the soft sounds of the men sleeping. The stars were hidden by cloud. The darkness was complete. I had never felt so far from the undercroft, from the grinding stone of my father’s voice. The world was vast and indifferent, and the rules that held the manor together were written on water out here. Edric knew it. Hugh knew it. I was just learning.
  - The next two days were a blur of aching travel. The land changed, the woods thinning, the tracks growing wider and more travelled. We passed other merchants, pilgrims, a party of monks on palfreys. The air began to carry a new smell—not earth and leaf, but smoke on a grand scale, and something else, something ripe and human and teeming. The smell of countless lives piled upon one another.

Then, on the afternoon of the third day, we crested a low rise, and I saw it.

London.

It was not a city as I understood it. It was a beast. A great, grey, sprawling beast couched in a bend of the river, its skin a patchwork of thatch and tile and timber, pierced by a hundred church spires like broken ribs. A pall of smoke hung over it, a breath of cooked meat, dung, and burning coal. The river itself was a crawling artery, thick with ships, their masts a leafless forest. The walls were a distant, jagged line, but the life of the place spilled beyond them, a chaos of buildings and noise that rolled out towards us.

I stood on the cart bench, heedless of propriety, my hand gripping the wood until my knuckles whitened. The sheer size of it was a physical blow. My world was the manor, the village, the abbey. This was a different creation. Here, my father’s name was nothing. His honour was a local coin, worthless here. Here, everything was for sale.

Hugh grunted beside me. “The Beast. It chews up boys like your brother and spits out the bones.” He pointed towards the river, east of the walls. “The Steelyard. The Flemish quarter. That is where we are bound.”

We joined a river of traffic flowing towards the city gates—carts like ours, wagons laden with stone and timber, herds of bleating sheep, people on foot in clothes of every cut and colour. The noise rose around us—shouts, curses, the bleat of animals, the creak of wheels, the distant clang of a hundred hammers. The air was thick with dust and stench.

At the Aldgate, guards in the city’s livery waved us through with a cursory glance at our load. The toll had been paid upstream. And then we were inside.

The world closed in, loud and violent and immediate. The streets were narrow canyons, the buildings leaning in so close their upper stories almost kissed. The ground was a foul slurry of mud and refuse. People jostled everywhere, their faces sharp and strange. A preacher screamed damnation from a barrel. A hawker shoved pies under my nose. A legless man dragged himself through the muck, holding out a wooden bowl. The smell was overwhelming—the reek of the tanneries, the sweetness of rotting fruit, the tang of spilled ale, the underlying stench of too many bodies in too small a space.

I shrank back on the cart, my earlier resolve crumbling to dust. This was the heart of the danger. Not a physical peril, but a spiritual one. This chaos was what Edric wanted. This anonymity. This freedom from the weight of legacy and land. Looking at it, feeling its chaotic pulse, I understood him for the first time, and the understanding was a kind of terror. This was the cliff. And we had arrived at its very edge.

Hugh navigated the chaos with a grim focus, turning the carts down ever-smaller lanes that led towards the river. The noise of the city softened here, replaced by the cry of gulls and the slap of water against stone. We emerged onto a wide, cobbled wharf.

And there it was. The Steelyard. A fortified enclave within the city, its high walls separate from the London stone. From behind its gates came the smell of spices and new cloth and the clean, sharp scent of money. Men in fine, dark Flemish woollens moved purposefully in and out. The signs were in a foreign script.

Hugh pulled the carts to a halt in the shadow of the wall. He jumped down, his face set. “Wait here. Do not move. Do not speak to anyone. This is where the sharp business gets its edge.”

He strode to a small door in the great gate, spoke to a guard, and disappeared inside.

I sat on the cart, in the shadow of the Beast, the weight of the wool at my back, the weight of the city before me. I had journeyed to the antagonist’s stronghold. But the antagonist was not a man behind these walls. It was the choice itself. It was the future, raw and hungry and smelling of the river.

I had come to witness. Now I had to decide what I would bear witness to. The successful sale? The proof of Edric’s dream? Or the moment the last thread between my brother and my father was cut, here on these foreign stones, by my own silent consent?

Hugh returned, and with him came a tall, thin man with a face like a ledger page—pale, precise, and devoid of warmth. The factor. He carried a small, iron-bound chest.

The bargaining was about to begin.



---

Chapter 5

Chapter 5

The factor’s name was De Vries. He did not offer it; Hugh spoke it in the introduction, a single coin dropped onto a counter. De Vries acknowledged it with a blink, his eyes the colour of a winter creek, all stone-bed and shallow light. He looked at the carts, at the wool, and finally at me. His gaze was an assessment of tare and tret, calculating the percentage of useless boy-flesh bundled on the driver’s bench. He found it wanting.

“The sample,” he said. His voice was a dry rustle, like ledger pages turning.

Hugh, his own face a closed fist, moved to the nearest bale. He cut the cords with his belt knife, the hemp parting with a sigh. He pulled a handful of fleece, its grey-brown cloud still holding the shape of the sheep’s flank, and offered it. De Vries took it. He did not feel it for weight or softness. He brought it to his nose and inhaled, his thin nostrils pinching. He was smelling for damp-rot, for the sour taint of a poorly cured skin. He smelled the moorland wind in it, the gorse and the peat. He let the wool fall back onto the bale as if discarding a used rag.

“The grade is variable,” De Vries stated. It was not a complaint, merely a fact entered into the invisible column of his mind.

“It’s good wool,” Hugh said, the words ground out. “Clean. From our own flocks.”

“Your own flocks are not a standard. The market is a standard.” De Vries turned his creek-bed eyes to the iron-bound chest he had placed on the cobbles. “We will weigh. We will agree a price per sack. You will accept it.”

It was not a negotiation. It was a pronouncement. This was the sharp business, and its edge was not honed here on the wharf; it was honed in Antwerp, in Bruges, in the silent counting-houses where the fate of northern sheep was decided between sips of Rhenish wine. Hugh knew it. I saw the knowledge settle on him like a yoke. He gave a single, stiff nod.

The weighing was a ritual of humiliation. De Vries produced a great set of scales. His guards, men with the broad, impassive faces of North Germans, began to unload the carts. They handled the sacks with a brute efficiency, slinging them onto the scale’s hook as if they were raw ore. De Vries called the weights, and a clerk with ink-stained fingers marked them in his book. Each call was a verdict. Each scratch of the quill was a sentence.

I sat on the empty cart, the Beast’s brass rings cold under my thighs. I watched the wealth of our land, the year’s labour of sun and rain and shepherd’s care, reduced to numbers in a foreign script. The smell of it, that familiar, lanolin-rich scent, was swallowed by the river-stink and the spice-dust from the Steelyard gates. This was the transaction Edric had dreamed of. The direct line. The cut-flower freedom from the old soil. But watching it, I felt no freedom. I felt a severing. A quiet, methodical unmaking.

Hugh stood like a stone beside the scale, his arms crossed. He did not look at the wool. He watched De Vries’s mouth, as if trying to read the numbers on his thin lips before they were spoken.

The last sack was weighed. The clerk’s quill paused. De Vries made a small, wet click with his tongue, a mental calculation complete. He looked at Hugh.

“For the lot. One hundred and twenty marks sterling.”

The air left the wharf. The cry of the gulls seemed to snag on the high walls. One hundred and twenty marks. It was a fortune. It was an insult. It was less than half what the wool would fetch if sold through the Staplers in Calais, after their tariffs and fees and greased palms. But it was cash. Now. On the spot. It was the price of anonymity.

Hugh’s face did not change, but a muscle in his jaw began to pulse, a tiny, trapped heartbeat. He was doing his own sums. The wages owed to the men who had guarded the train. The bribes paid on the road. The debt to the shadowy creditors in York. The sum left for Edric, to plant his flag in this new, rootless world. It would be enough. Just. It was the price of the cliff.

I saw it then, the devastating defeat. It was not in a sword-stroke or a shouted curse. It was in Hugh’s silence. It was in the way his broad shoulders, which had carried the weight of my father’s disappointment for a decade, seemed to hollow under an invisible burden. He was the steward. His life was a ledger of duty. And here, on these foreign stones, he was about to enter the figure that would zero out his purpose. He would enable the escape he could never permit himself. He would become a line-item in Edric’s rebellion.

He opened his mouth to speak the word of acceptance.

And I heard my father’s voice, a low, grinding stone from the undercroft. A man who cannot sharpen his own tools is a man who cannot feed his own people.

“No.”

The word was out of me before I knew I had thought it. It was not loud. It was flat, final, a stone dropped into a still pool.

Hugh’s head snapped towards me. His eyes were wide, not with anger, but with shock, as if one of the wool-sacks had spoken.

De Vries turned his pale face slowly, as if noticing a stain on the cobbles that had now acquired a voice. “The boy is unwell,” he said to Hugh, a statement of logistical inconvenience.

“I am not a boy,” I said. The terror was there, a cold river in my gut, but my voice did not shake. It found the flat, hard register of the whetstone on the blade. “And that is not the price.”

Hugh took a step towards me, his hand coming up. “Thomas—” It was a warning, a plea.

I stood on the cart bench, feeling the eyes of the guards upon me. I was the useless boy-flesh. I was the witness. But to bear witness was not to be a stone. It was to be a register. And I registered this: the factor’s haste, his lack of true haggling. The guarded, almost hungry way his men had handled the sacks. The smell of our wool, stronger and sweeter than any bale I’d seen off-loaded from other wagons.

“The Flemish looms are idle,” I said, the words coming not from my own mind, but from the memory of my father at table, grumbling over a month-old letter from a factor in Ghent. “The Italian ships are late. The raw wool in their warehouses is coarse, from the southern markets. Ours is finer. Longer staple.” I pointed at the opened bale. “You smelled it. You know the grade is not variable. It is prime. And you are desperate for it.”

De Vries’s expression did not alter, but the stillness around his eyes changed. The shallow light in them hardened, like ice over a creek. “You are a child playing with things you do not understand. The market price is set.”

“The market price is what a man will pay to keep his machines fed,” I said. “And your machines are hungry. One hundred and eighty marks.”

I heard Hugh’s sharp intake of breath. It was a wild, reckless figure. More than the Staplers would yield.

For the first time, De Vries looked at me. Truly looked. The assessment was no longer of tare and tret, but of threat. “You are not the principal. Your guardian has the authority.” He turned back to Hugh. “One hundred and twenty. The offer stands for the count of ten. Then it is one hundred and ten. This is not a barnyard auction.”

Hugh was staring at me. The world had narrowed to the space between us on the filthy cobbles. In his face, I saw the crumbling of a different resolve. He had come here to complete an errand, to endure a defeat in service to a lost cause. He had not come to fight. I was offering a fight. I was offering the one thing he knew how to do.

He looked from my face to De Vries’s. I saw the steward return. The man who bargained with bad harvests and murrain and hard winters. The man who sharpened his own tools.

“The boy speaks for the principal,” Hugh said, his voice finding its old, gravelly authority. “And he’s right. You need this wool. One hundred and seventy.”

The haggling that followed was a silent, vicious thing. No raised voices. No gestures. Just figures tossed between them like knife-throws. One sixty-five. One fifty-five. One fifty. Back and forth. De Vries’s pale cheeks gained two spots of colour, like a fever. Hugh stood his ground, a rock in the river of the factor’s contempt.

My legs were trembling. I locked my knees. I had thrown a stone into the mechanism, and now the gears were grinding, and I had no idea what the machine would do.

Finally, De Vries hissed a word. “One hundred forty. Final. Or you take your stinking wool back to your barbarian hills.”

Hugh looked at me. A question. A passing of the tool.

It was still a cut-price. Still a betrayal of the old ways. But it was twenty marks more than the first offer. Twenty marks that was the difference between Edric’s clean start and a start still mired in debt. It was a compromise written in coin. It was the only victory this ground would yield.

I gave a single, slight nod.

“Done,” Hugh said.

De Vries turned away, snapping his fingers at the clerk. The iron-bound chest was opened. It was not full of coin; that would be a fool’s errand. It held sealed purses of gold nobles and silver pennies, and a sheaf of bills of exchange from the Medici bank, good as gold in any trading port in Christendom. Hugh counted it with a meticulous, furious slowness, his big fingers deft on the parchment and the leather. The weight of the wool was gone from the carts. Now a new weight, metallic and promissory, settled in its place.

When the chest was closed and locked, De Vries fixed me with one last look. “You have the sharp tongue of a usurer, boy. A pity. The land needs blunt men.”

He and his guards retreated into the Steelyard, the great gate closing with a soft, definitive thud of oak and iron.

The silence they left behind was immense. Hugh stood over the chest, his hands on its lid. He did not speak for a long time. The river slapped the wharf. A cart rumbled in the distance.

“You should not have done that,” he said finally, not looking at me.

“I know.”

“He could have refused the lot. Called the guards. We’d be on the road back with nothing but trouble to show for it.”

“I know.”

He turned then. His face was weary, etched with lines I had not seen before. But his eyes were clear. “Why did you?”

The answer was there, in the cold air, in the smell of the sold wool that still clung to our clothes. “Because a man who cannot sharpen his own tools cannot feed his own people,” I said. “And these are our people. Edric is. However far he runs.”

Hugh studied me. He nodded, once. It was not approval. It was recognition. “Get down. We need to get this chest off the open wharf.”

We transferred the chest to the smaller, covered cart—the one that had carried our provisions. It fitted under the driver’s bench. We covered it with sacking. It looked like nothing. It was everything.

We did not speak as Hugh turned the carts, the empty ones now rattling with a forlorn, hollow sound. We moved back into the labyrinth, away from the river’s edge. The victory, such as it was, felt cold and stolen. We had out-bargained the factor, but we had still sold the wool. The thread was still cut. The deed was done.

Hugh navigated not back towards the city gate, but deeper into the warren of lanes around Eastcheap. He had a place in mind, a tavern he knew from earlier trips with my father, where a man could pay for silence with a few extra pennies. The afternoon was fading, the sky a dirty wool-grey above the crooked rooftops.

We were in a narrow alley, the buildings leaning in so close a man could almost touch both sides, when the trap was sprung.

It was not a dramatic ambush. A handcart appeared from a courtyard ahead, pushed by a ragged man, its wheel coming loose, spilling a load of kindling across the alley’s width. Hugh cursed, pulling up the horses. As he did, I heard the soft scuff of boots behind us.

I turned. Three men blocked the way we had come. They were not guards. They were London rats—lean, hard-faced, with eyes that held no light. One held a cudgel. Another, a long knife. The third, the leader, had nothing in his hands. He had a face pocked like a thimble-top and a smile that showed brown teeth.

“Heavy carts, friend,” the leader said, his voice a nasal whine. “Long road. Time to lighten your load.”

Hugh was already moving, his own knife coming out from his belt. “There’s nothing for you here. Wool’s sold. We’re empty.”

“Aye, the wool’s sold,” the rat-man said, taking a step forward. “We saw. From the roof by the wharf. Saw the little iron chest go in your cart. Very careful, you were. Very heavy.”

My blood turned to ice. They had watched the whole transaction. They had waited for this moment, this pinch-point in the city’s gut.

“That chest is not yours,” Hugh said, his voice low and deadly. He stepped down from the cart, placing himself between them and me. “Walk away. Last chance.”

The leader laughed, a sound like gravel shaken in a cup. “Big man from the big hills. This ain’t your pasture.”

He nodded. The man with the cudgel lunged.

What followed was not a knightly duel. It was a brief, brutal explosion of violence in the stinking alley. Hugh met the cudgel-man with a grunt, taking the blow on his raised forearm with a sickening crack, but driving his own knife into the man’s belly. The man shrieked, folding. The knife-man came in low and fast. Hugh kicked out, catching his knee, and the man went down with a curse.

But the leader was the danger. He did not rush. He circled as the others engaged, his eyes on me. On the covered cart.

I scrambled down, my own dagger in my hand—a small, useless thing against these street-wolves. The leader saw it and his grin widened. He feinted towards Hugh, then suddenly darted past the struggling men, straight for the cart’s bench.

He was going for the chest.

I moved without thought. I threw myself at him, not with the knife, but with my whole weight, hitting him sideways. We crashed into the side of the cart. The breath left him in a whoof. He snarled, an animal sound, and backhanded me across the face. Light exploded behind my eyes. I tasted blood. I fell to the cobbles, the world tilting.

I saw Hugh, his back to me, finishing with the knife-man. I saw the leader, his brown-toothed smile gone, now a rictus of greed, reach under the bench and drag the iron-bound chest out. It was too heavy to run with. He braced himself, heaving it into his arms.

And I saw my chance. Not at him. At the cart. At the Beast.

My father’s words were a drumbeat in my skull. God gave you hands, not just to pray with.

I grabbed the heavy brass ring of the brake lever. I pulled it with all the strength I had left.

The mechanism released with a metallic shriek. The cart, on the slight incline of the alley, lurched forward.

The leader, bent under the weight of the chest, his back to the cart, heard it too late. He started to turn. The Beast’s solid oak tailgate struck him square in the back.

There was a sound like a green branch snapping. He cried out, a high, surprised sound, and was driven forward, crushed between the moving cart and the stone wall of the alley. The chest fell from his grasp, hitting the cobbles with a deafening clang of iron. The cart ground to a halt, pinning him.

Silence, then. Broken by the moaning of the cudgel-man in the filth, and the ragged sound of Hugh’s breathing. The third man was unconscious. The leader was not moving, his body bent at a terrible angle.

Hugh staggered to me, hauling me up. “Are you hurt?”

I shook my head, spitting blood. My face was on fire. He looked past me to the pinned man, to the chest lying in the muck. His eyes came back to mine. There was no praise in them. There was a kind of horrified awe.

“Jesus, Thomas.”

He moved quickly then. He checked the leader—dead or dying, it did not matter. He righted the chest, heaving it back onto the cart with a groan of effort. He dragged the two living assailants into the courtyard from which the handcart had come. He came back, his face grey.

“We go. Now. Before the watch comes for the noise.”

We left the alley. We left the broken man pinned by the Beast. We left a part of myself there too, crushed on those stones. I had prayed with my hands. And I had killed with them.

The tavern was a low, smoky den called The Star in the East. The landlord, a man with a melted-looking face and one good eye, took Hugh’s extra pennies and asked no questions. He gave us a room at the back, overlooking a midden-heap. It had a stout door and a bar for the inside.

We carried the chest up. When the door was barred, Hugh slumped onto a stool, cradling his left arm. It was already swelling, a purple bruise rising from wrist to elbow. He looked at the chest, then at me, sitting on the narrow pallet, my whole body beginning to shake.

“The price,” he said, his voice hollow. “This is the price of the sharp business. Not just the numbers. The blood on the stones. You see now? You see what your brother’s dream is built on?”

I saw. I saw the factor’s cold eyes. I saw the rat-man’s grin vanishing in terror. I saw the Beast, my faithful, stupid cart, become an instrument of death. I had come to witness. I had witnessed the market’s hunger, and the violence that fed it. I had sought to sharpen the tool, and I had drawn blood.

“We have the money,” I said, my voice a stranger’s. “We completed the task.”

“Aye,” Hugh said. He laughed, a short, bitter sound. “We completed the task. And what are we now? Stewards? Or mercenaries? Men of business? Or men of blood?” He looked at his swollen arm. “Your father… he would know what to do with a man who attacked his own on the road. He’d hang him from the oak by the gatehouse. A lesson. Clear. Clean. This… this city filth. This is Edric’s new world. No oaks. Just alleys. And no lessons, just bodies in the dark.”

He was right. And he was wrong. My father’s world had its own filth, its own hidden violences. But they were ordered. They had a name, a place, a reason. This was chaos. This was the cliff.

And I had stepped over it.

I stood up. My legs held. I went to the chest. I knelt before it. I did not open it. I put my hands on its cold iron bands.

“This isn’t for Edric,” I said.

Hugh went still. “What?”

“The money. This isn’t for his dream. Not anymore.” I looked up at him. The understanding was upon me, cold and clear and terrible. It was the reward for surviving the alley. Not the chest. The insight. “He wanted to sell the wool to be free of the land. To be free of the name. But this…” I slapped the lid. “This came from the land. It was bought with blood on city stones. It is soaked in the name. He cannot be free of it. And if we give it to him, we make him a part of this… this anonymous filth. We make him the man in the alley, greedy and dead. We don’t save him. We bury him.”

Hugh stared at me as if I were speaking Greek. “Then what, in God’s name, do we do? We can’t take it back to your father. That would be…”

“It would be the truth,” I said. “It would be the sharpest tool of all.” The plan formed as I spoke it, jagged and dangerous. “We don’t go to Edric. We let him come to us. We use the money as he meant to use it. Not for escape. For a claim.”

“A claim? On what?”

“On the legacy he wants to burn. On the Lacy name.” I took a deep breath, the air raw in my throat. “We buy something. Not freedom. Responsibility. We take his rebellion and we turn it back on itself. We use his weapon to arm the thing he hates.”

Hugh was silent for a long minute. The noise of the tavern filtered through the floorboards—raucous laughter, the clatter of tankards. A world away.

“You would use his money to buy back into the very thing he’s selling?” he said slowly. “He’ll see it as the ultimate betrayal.”

“It is,” I said, the truth of it a stone in my chest. “But it’s the only betrayal that offers a future. For him. For the land. Otherwise, the money is just… blood-price. It vanishes in the chaos, and he vanishes with it. And we are just the men who delivered him to the rats.”

Hugh looked at his broken arm. He looked at the chest. He looked at me, my face bruised, my knuckles scraped, my eyes older than they had been that morning.

“God help us,” he whispered. But he nodded. “What do we buy?”

I told him. The idea had been seeded in me on the wharf, watching De Vries, watching the foreign wool merchants. It had grown in the alley, fed by violence. It was not my father’s way. It was not Edric’s way. It was a third path, built from the wreckage of both.

“We need a lawyer,” I said. “A London man. One who knows the courts of Chancery, not just the manor courts. And then we need to send word north. Not to my father. To the creditors in York. We have their money. We’ll pay them. But on new terms.”

“Your brother will never agree.”

“He doesn’t have to agree,” I said. “Not yet. He just has to come and face what his freedom has bought. He has to look at it. As I had to look at the cliff.”

Hugh leaned back, closing his eyes. The steward in him was wrestling with the soldier, the loyal retainer with the pragmatic survivor. The survivor won.

“We stay in this hole tonight,” he said. “Tomorrow, I find a surgeon for this arm. And you… you stay barred in this room with the chest. Tomorrow, we find our lawyer.” He opened his eyes. They were full of a weary resolve. “You have set us on a course, Thomas de Lacy. There is no turning back now. You understand? You have taken the tiller.”

I understood. I had confronted the enemy—the faceless, hungry chaos. I had suffered a defeat—the death of my own innocence on the alley stones. I had overcome—not with a sword, but with the lever of a cart and a colder, harder purpose. And my reward was this: not gold, but agency. The terrible, weighty power to choose the shape of the future.

I was no longer just a witness.

I was the instrument.

And as I sat in that foul room, the iron chest at my feet, the groans of the wounded city all around us, I knew the hardest task was yet to come. I would have to face my brother. I would have to show him the future I had purchased with his dream. I would have to see the hatred in his eyes, and know that I had earned it.

The hook was set. Not in the city’s flesh, but in my own. And the line led back north, to where Edric waited, dreaming of a clean break. I was about to show him how deeply the barb was buried.



---

Chapter 6

Chapter 6

The lawyer’s name was Fulk, and he smelled of dried lavender and old parchment, a sharp, academic scent that seemed to push the tavern’s reek of sour ale and unwashed bodies a few inches back from the table. He was a small man, built of angles and tendons, with eyes the colour of a winter puddle and a voice that was a dry, precise instrument. He listened. That was the first thing. He did not interrupt, or scoff, or lean forward with avarice gleaming in his puddle-eyes. He simply listened, his long-fingered hands steepled before him, as I laid out the geometry of our ruin.

Hugh sat beside me, his left arm bound tight to his chest in a sling of clean linen. The surgeon, a pinch-faced man with blood under his nails, had called it a ‘greenstick fracture’ and charged us a silver penny for the privilege of the name and the binding. The pain was a live coal in Hugh’s face, but he held himself still, a testament to the steward’s discipline, his good hand resting on the tabletop like a stone paperweight.

I told Fulk everything. The debt. The wool. Edric’s flight. The ambush in the alley. The chest. I did not speak of the cliff, or the choice, or the feeling of the iron lever in my hands. Those were not facts for a lawyer’s ledger. I gave him the numbers, the names from the York creditors, the weight of the silver marks in the chest. I gave him the corpse of our circumstance.

When I finished, Fulk blinked once, slowly. He unsteepled his hands and laid them flat on the scarred wood. “You wish to discharge the primary obligations, but renegotiate the terms of the secondary pledges. Your brother’s lands, entailed though they are, were used as surety. You wish to convert that surety into a graduated repayment, secured against the future income of the wool, not the land itself.”

“I wish to keep my family from being turned out of their home by a money-lender from York,” I said.

“A laudable sentiment,” Fulk replied, his tone devoid of any sentiment at all. “Legally, it is a matter of substitution. You offer a new, more liquid asset—the silver, the promise of the wool—in place of the illiquid one—the land. The creditors may accept, if they perceive the risk of default on the land to be greater than the risk of delay on the wool. Given your brother’s current reputation as a fugitive from debt, they may be amenable. The Chancery can draft a writ of cessavit, but we would seek a covenant before it comes to that.”

He spoke a different language. It was not the blunt, physical tongue of the farm, of scythes and yields and weather. It was not the silent, bloody grammar of the alley. It was a language of air and ink, of consequences deferred and reshaped. It felt both powerful and terribly fragile.

“Can it be done?” Hugh grunted.

“It can be attempted,” Fulk corrected. “The silver you have recovered is the key. It demonstrates capacity. It is a gesture of good faith. Without it, you would be petitioners begging for mercy. With it, you are negotiators proposing a new contract.” His eyes flicked to the heavy box at my feet. “You have it here?”

“A sample,” I said. I bent, the muscles of my back protesting like rusty hinges, and opened the chest. The smell of cold iron and old fear rose from it. I lifted one of the smaller purses, its leather gone stiff, and placed it on the table. It landed with a solid, incontestable thud.

Fulk did not reach for it. He observed it, as a physician might observe a symptom. “This was acquired in the redress of a felony.”

It was not a question. I said nothing.

“The Crown has an interest in recovered stolen property,” he continued. “A percentage, often a third, is typically owed to the victim, a third to the Crown, a third to the apprehendor. Your position as both victim and apprehendor is… murky. We will not volunteer this history. But if it is discovered, it will complicate matters. Significantly.”

“The men who stole it are dead,” Hugh said, his voice low. “The one who ordered it is dead. There is no one to dispute its provenance.”

“The dead are silent,” Fulk agreed. “But silence can be interrogated. A coroner’s roll. A rumour from the sheriff’s men. London is a web, Master Hugh. A tremor in one strand is felt in all the others.” He finally reached out and pulled the purse towards him. He did not open it. He weighed it in his palm. “We will use it. But quietly. We will convert it into bills of exchange through a Lombard I know. A man of discreet priorities.”

He laid out his plan. It was a thing of steps and stages, of documents sealed and messengers sent, of waiting upon the law’s slow, grinding wheels. It would take weeks. Months, perhaps. We would need to stay in London, or near to it. Hugh’s face grew longer with each phase. He was a man of harvests and seasons, of tasks that began at dawn and were resolved by dusk. This legal husbandry, this planting of parchment in the hope of a future harvest, was an agony to him.

“And my brother?” I asked, when Fulk paused.

The lawyer’s gaze settled on me, and for the first time, I saw a flicker of something that was not pure calculation. It might have been pity. “You wish to include him in this covenant?”

“I wish him to understand it. To see it.”

“That is a familial matter, not a legal one. Legally, his consent is not required for you to act as steward of the de Lacy assets, given your father’s infirmity and his own… absence. Morally,” he gave a slight, bony shrug, “that is your own country. I do not map it.”

“I need to send for him. To bring him here.”

Fulk considered. “A summons from a brother may be ignored. A summons from the King’s Chancery, in relation to a matter of debt and property, may not. I can have a writ drawn. Venire facias. It would compel his appearance before an examiner. It would not be… gentle.”

The hook, turning in the flesh. Not my line, but the law’s. I felt a cold nausea at the elegance of it. “Do it,” I said.

Hugh shifted beside me. “Thomas…”

“Do it,” I repeated, looking at Fulk.

The lawyer nodded, made a minute note on a wax tablet with a stylus. “It will be done. Now, my terms.” He named his fee. It was a number that would have made my father spit. It was a fraction of what we would save. I agreed. The silver in the purse before him was his retainer.

When the business was concluded, Fulk stood. He was even smaller upright. “I will begin today. You have lodging?”

“The Goat and Horn,” Hugh said, the name sounding foul in his mouth.

“Adequate. Stay there. Be dull. Do not buy fine clothes. Do not drink in better taverns. Be invisible men, nursing a sick relative. I will send word.” He bowed his head, a precise dip of the chin, and was gone, leaving the scent of lavender to be swallowed by the tavern’s thicker gloom.

Hugh let out a long, slow breath. “That is a creature of the new world.”

“He is the tool we need,” I said, but the words tasted like ash. A tool had no pity in its eyes.

“Aye. And we have put our necks in its mechanism.” He pushed himself up, his face paling at the movement of his arm. “Christ, I need an ale that doesn’t taste of despair.”
  - We did not celebrate. There was no feast, no raised cups. But that evening, in the fug of the Goat and Horn’s common room, we achieved a kind of grim armistice with our situation. We had a plan. It was a labyrinthine, paper plan, but it was a path. Hugh drank steadily, seeking a numbness the surgeon’s draught had not provided. I drank to keep him company, the cheap, thin ale doing little but filling my belly with a sour weight.

We had taken a better room. It was not good, but it had a bar for the door and a window that did not stink directly of the midden. The chest was under my pallet. Its presence was a cold tumour in the room.

“Your father would call that man a parasite,” Hugh said, staring into his cup. “A tick that feeds on the disputes of his betters.”

“My father is not here,” I said. “And the tick has a solution.”

“Aye. For a price. Everything has a price.” He looked at me, his eyes red-rimmed from pain and drink. “You did what was needed in that alley, Thomas. You saved the silver. You saved us. But you crossed a river there. You feel it, don’t you? The current. It’s got you now.”

I thought of the lever, of the crunch of bone giving way. I thought of the cold, clear purpose that had filled me, scouring out the fear. It had not left me. It sat inside, a quiet, watchful thing. “I feel it.”

“It will get easier,” he said, but it was not a comfort. It was a warning. “The next choice. And the next. They will come quicker. They will feel less like choices and more like necessities.
